    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_7613232">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7613232" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/472694-e" class="left imgcol" title="E"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1201447841p2/472694.jpg" alt="E" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/7613232?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 20, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/472694-e" class="user" title="E">E</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/233633.Little_House_on_the_Prairie" title="Hardcover 9780060264451" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/472694-e?shelf=childrensfiction" class="actionLinkLite">childrensfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer7613232">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17534957593559834625">Okay, it's a great American classic, I realize that. I read it for the first time in third grade because the pioneer-go-forth-and-push-westward philosophy is a central feature in the proud American mindset and heritage. But it's for that very reason that the value of the book needs to be questioned.<br /><br />While much of the story focuses on a family's self-reliance on the Kansas prairie, the book preceding it - <em>Little House in the Big Woods</em> - does the same with the exception that the Ingalls family was</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17534957593559834625">Okay, it's a great American classic, I realize that. I read it for the first time in third grade because the pioneer-go-forth-and-push-westward philosophy is a central feature in the proud American mindset and heritage. But it's for that very reason that the value of the book needs to be questioned.<br /><br />While much of the story focuses on a family's self-reliance on the Kansas prairie, the book preceding it - <em>Little House in the Big Woods</em> - does the same with the exception that the Ingalls family was integrated into a functioning Wisconsin community of relatives and neighbors. That book, however, is NOT the famous one after which a television series was made. <br /><br />WHY the Ingalls family felt the need to abandon their community and settle in what was in fact disputed Indian Territory other than out of a lust for adventure is insufficiently explained. Unlike immigrants of the time, American pioneers like the Ingallses were not driven to the new land by persecution or famine at home. They drove themselves there and expected the local Indians to like it or stay out of the way. The Indians are portrayed as mysterious savages who are ultimately given what actually belonged to the hard-working white family. (I'm not at all surprised it was written in the 1930's.) <br /><br />My third grade class was outraged at the injustice of the U.S. government telling the Ingallses' to abandon their self-made cabin for the Indians, yet no one was outraged in the beginning when they arrived and no one was asked to question this. Stories like the Ingallses's are history that cannot be changed or forgotten, but like all history should be constantly questioned. <br /><br />I would read this to children and elementary/middle school classes, but not without a corresponding story from the perspective of the Plains Indians, and not without asking children important follow-up questions to spark dialogue. Did the Ingallses have to leave Wisconsin? Would you have? Why do you think they decided to? Were the Ingallses malicious, naive, or justified in their pursuit? Can the rural dislike of government involvement be traced back to stories like theirs? Why was this story so popular in the 1930's, 40's, and 50's? Why is it still popular today?<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17534957593559834625">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17713166503006945805"><em>Athalia wrote: "Actually to clarify, according to "Pioneer Girl..." Laura had been born in Wisconsin, but moved to Kansas with her family when she was</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17713166503006945805"><em>Athalia wrote: "Actually to clarify, according to "Pioneer Girl..." Laura had been born in Wisconsin, but moved to Kansas with her family when she was a year old."</em><br />I've been reading a bunch of different books regarding LIW. Yes, Laura wouldn't have remembered Kansas since she was only a few years old. She set the books so the character of Laura was a few years older. ALso, in the latter books, Almanzo wasn't only just a few years older than Laura, in real life he was a full 10 years older.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_716684676">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/716684676" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/279256-diane" class="left imgcol" title="Diane"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1404330313p2/279256.jpg" alt="Diane" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/716684676?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 14, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/279256-diane" class="user" title="Diane">Diane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=midwest" class="actionLinkLite">midwest</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=racism" class="actionLinkLite">racism</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=pioneers" class="actionLinkLite">pioneers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer716684676">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4793810376608572334">I have mixed feelings about this book. My mother read the Laura Ingalls books with me when I was a little girl, and I'm rereading them for the first time in 30 years. <br /><br />"Little House on the Prairie" is the story of the Ingalls family -- Pa and Ma, Laura, her sister Mary and her baby sister Carrie -- taking a covered wagon all the way from Wisconsin to Kansas at about 1870. The author is vague on the timing, such as exactly what year it was or how old she was, but it seems to be written from the pe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4793810376608572334">I have mixed feelings about this book. My mother read the Laura Ingalls books with me when I was a little girl, and I'm rereading them for the first time in 30 years. <br /><br />"Little House on the Prairie" is the story of the Ingalls family -- Pa and Ma, Laura, her sister Mary and her baby sister Carrie -- taking a covered wagon all the way from Wisconsin to Kansas at about 1870. The author is vague on the timing, such as exactly what year it was or how old she was, but it seems to be written from the perspective of a 6-year-old. I read in a biography that she played a little loose with the timeline. <br /><br />Once they reach Kansas, which was still Indian Territory back then, the family finds a nice bit of prairie and builds a log cabin and starts growing crops. But after a year there, Pa gets upset at news that Washington has decided to move the white settlers out of Indian Territory, and the family abruptly packs up the wagon and leaves the cabin behind, heading back to Wisconsin.<br /><br />I'll start with what I liked about the book, which is the story of the wagon trip and the actual homesteading. I have admiration for all the brave pioneers and immigrants and travelers of the world who set out with very few belongings and started a new life somewhere else, surviving on their wits and the kindness of strangers. <br /><br />"'We're going to do well here, Caroline,' Pa said. 'This is great country. This is a country I'll be contented to stay in the rest of my life.'<br /><br />'Even when it's settled up?' Ma asked.<br /><br />'Even when it's settled up. No matter how thick and close the neighbors get, this country'll never feel crowded. Look at that sky!'"<br /><br />There were some lovely heartwarming moments, such as the Christmas dinner that was saved with the help of a neighbor who met Santa and carried the children's gifts to them. Or their loyal dog Jack, who survived a near-fatal river crossing. The book is filled with charming illustrations, and I had a vivid memory of some of the pictures.<br /><br />It was fascinating to see the series of steps that Pa and Ma took to frame a house, dig a well, build a barn, make a fireplace, save seeds to grow crops, etc. Each event was a big deal and was an adventure. For example, Pa and a neighbor nearly died while digging the well because some underground gas almost poisoned them. And the whole family got sick with malaria one summer, but luckily a doctor in the territory saved them in time. Then there was the day of a massive prairie fire and only quick thinking by Pa &amp; Ma saved the house. Or the time there was a panther on the prairie who was tracking the family, but luckily an Indian killed it before it attacked anyone.<br /><br />Which brings me to what I didn't like about the book, which was racism against the Indians. I honestly do not remember all the racist comments from my childhood reading. The line, "The only good Indian is a dead Indian," is mentioned several times. Or how Ma was constantly fretting about them, saying, "Land knows, they'd never do anything with this country themselves. All they do is roam around over it like wild animals. Treaties or no treaties, the land belongs to folks that'll farm it. That's only common sense and justice." Did my mother skip those parts when she read these books with me? Or did it just not register? I understand it was the prevailing attitude of the whites at the time, but it was jarring to read it in a children's book, even one that was first published in 1935. I think this is important because if I were going to read this book with my niece, for example, I would edit out the racist parts, which is probably what my mom did. I don't see the need to plant the seeds of an old prejudice in a young child. <br /><br />I struggled with whether to give this book a 3 or a 4, but I rounded up out of affection for the series as a whole. The first book in the series, "Little House in the Big Woods," has been my favorite so far. But I will continue to read these books and enjoy the stories of the early settlers.<br /><br />I'll close with this nice thought from Laura toward the end of the book when the family is leaving the homestead behind: "Laura felt all excited inside. You never know what will happen next, nor where you'll be tomorrow, when you are traveling in a covered wagon."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4793810376608572334">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="xhr_comment_body">
    <a href="/user/show/1914029-cristina" style="font-weight:bold">Cristina</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer6045274067822177566">I think it's important to remain true to the text while ensuring that the disturbing language and attitudes open up a discussion with your child. How</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6045274067822177566">I think it's important to remain true to the text while ensuring that the disturbing language and attitudes open up a discussion with your child. How can we teach children history if we white wash everything? The history of this continent is messy, and many times ugly. And unfortunately some of these opinions about race are still held by people in our own society. If we're going to read to our kids about certain events we should be ready to have discussions with them about the choices people make and the opinions people held/hold.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5014803115274115268">We only see the situation through Laura's eyes. I'm sure in the Indian camp there were children who heard their parents say "The only good white man is a dead one." Unfortunately, we can't change history. We have to accept that attitudes were different in the past and evolve over time. I'm sure that 100 years from now nobody will eat meat and will look back at our butcher shops and barbecues and say that we were barbarians.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_22121595">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22121595" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/870333-laurel-wicke" class="left imgcol" title="Laurel Wicke"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1377105406p2/870333.jpg" alt="Laurel Wicke" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/22121595?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 22, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/870333-laurel-wicke" class="user" title="Laurel Wicke">Laurel Wicke</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/870333-laurel-wicke?shelf=read-to-logan" class="actionLinkLite">read-to-logan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer22121595">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6282896083281630601">I am a fan of the Laura Ingall's Wilder books, and I am enjoying them even more as an adult, sharing them with my daughter. This one moved a bit more slowly than Little House in the Big Woods, but I was still fascinated. I can hardly imagine a life so primitive. Some say Pa was crazy for moving his family away from the Big Woods where they had a solid footing, but the settler's spirit is responsible for the growth and development of our country and is still the heart of the American way. Who doe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6282896083281630601">I am a fan of the Laura Ingall's Wilder books, and I am enjoying them even more as an adult, sharing them with my daughter. This one moved a bit more slowly than Little House in the Big Woods, but I was still fascinated. I can hardly imagine a life so primitive. Some say Pa was crazy for moving his family away from the Big Woods where they had a solid footing, but the settler's spirit is responsible for the growth and development of our country and is still the heart of the American way. Who doesn't look to better the situation of their family even when it means stepping into the unknown? Mrs. Wilder's detailed descriptions of the hard work and difficulties fill me with awe and respect. It was such a different world in so many ways, but still I can relate to the deep family love and commitment that shines through on every page.<br /><br />A word about some reviews knocking her for her politically incorrect treatment of Native Americans: We can and should be disturbed by history. It can at times seem unfair, ugly, and even wrong; however, ignoring it or rewriting it to suit our current cultural standard is ignorant. The truth is, during this period of history there are no easy answers regarding settlers and Indians. There was fear, distrust, and wrong-doings on both sides. Her description is historically accurate. It is how she really saw it. I do not think we would do our children any service by sugar-coating history as some reviewers seem to argue. It is by struggling with the injustices of the past that we invaluably inform our judgements and actions regarding the complex questions of today. Please let us not rob our children of the hard truth. Let us give them knowledge and experience and arm them to do better and be better today. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6282896083281630601">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1172384293">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer105756548219662830">This is not really a review of the general contents and themes of <a title="Little House on the Prairie (Little House, #2) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77767.Little_House_on_the_Prairie__Little_House___2_">Little House on the Prairie</a>, but more my personal attitudes towards the fact that this book has been (and like so many others) repeatedly challenged and even at times banned/censored (mostly due to the way Native Americans are depicted and the attitudes shown towards them).<br /><br />There are definite issues with <u>Little House on the Prairie</u>, and especially the attitudes towards Native Americans are problematic to say the least. However, att</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText105756548219662830">This is not really a review of the general contents and themes of <a title="Little House on the Prairie (Little House, #2) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77767.Little_House_on_the_Prairie__Little_House___2_">Little House on the Prairie</a>, but more my personal attitudes towards the fact that this book has been (and like so many others) repeatedly challenged and even at times banned/censored (mostly due to the way Native Americans are depicted and the attitudes shown towards them).<br /><br />There are definite issues with <u>Little House on the Prairie</u>, and especially the attitudes towards Native Americans are problematic to say the least. However, attitudes towards Native Americans in the 19th century were often very much akin to those shown in the book, and the claim that the "only good Indian is a dead Indian" was unfortunately common amongst many settlers (like the Scotts, who I think, uttered these words). Those who would challenge these books and attempt to have them banned are thus not only being censorious, but even worse, are attempting to erase the unfortunate truth that Native Americans were often seen in this way. <u>Little House on the Prairie</u> would, in my opinion, be a good starting point for discussions, although I do realise the book could also be used and likely has been used by those against Native Americans to bolster and justify their own prejudices (however, this can be and has been the case with many, if not most controversial books, and will likely remain thus).<br /><br />Frankly, while I have more than a bit of trouble with the way Native Americans are depicted and the attitudes shown towards them (I especially find the often glowing descriptions of Caroline Ingalls a bit hard to stomach, as she is really quite the stereotyper, much more so than Charles Ingalls, although not on the same level as the Scotts), I do very much appreciate the fact that Laura Ingalls Wilder has not tried pretend that attitudes towards Native Americans were different and more positive (if she had, for example, written stories about the Ingalls family becoming close to and good friends with their Native American neighbours, although this might feel more acceptable to our modern sensibilities, it also would be, for the most part, woefully and perhaps even dangerously anachronistic). <br /><br />And finally, one must realise that while <u>Little House on the Prairie</u> is considered historical fiction, it was also written at a time when negative impressions of or at the very least patronising and Euro-centric attitudes to Native Americans were still very much not only acceptable, but common. The book describes the past, but is also of its time, and should be read, appreciated and approached as such.<br /><br />I also wonder, whether those individuals who believe that <u>Little House on the Prairie</u> should be banned and/or censored consider themselves to be educated, to be socially active, to be fighting against bigotry and the like (and the answer would probably be a resounding "yes" for most). However, if one strives to ban or censor books, one is behahving (and no matter for what reason one attempts to ban or censor a book) the same or at least in a similar manner as that against which one is fighting. Education, understanding, solidarity will never be reached, nor will the battle against bigotry ever be won by using similarly problematic (and draconian) means, but through thoughtful discussion and debate (in my opinion, banning books, no matter for what purpose, is and always will be an act of bigotry, an act of dictatorial over-reaching, an act that destroys freedom and only creates more strife). <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="105756548219662830">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer2687491">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16906596036535136049">I can vividly remember the first time I read this book. I was sleeping over at my best friend Mary's house when I was about seven or eight years old. She lived next door to me. Her family always slept with their attic fan on, and with a radio in each bedroom tuned in to a country station. This was strange to me, as nights at my house were totally quiet. Plus, I was a little freaked out at spending the night away from home, because I hadn't really done that very much at that point in my life. So,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16906596036535136049">I can vividly remember the first time I read this book. I was sleeping over at my best friend Mary's house when I was about seven or eight years old. She lived next door to me. Her family always slept with their attic fan on, and with a radio in each bedroom tuned in to a country station. This was strange to me, as nights at my house were totally quiet. Plus, I was a little freaked out at spending the night away from home, because I hadn't really done that very much at that point in my life. So, the noise and the mild homesickness added up to a sleepless night for little Bethie. So, after Mary went to sleep, I picked up "Little House on the Prairie" off her bookshelf. I'm not sure how she came to own that book, as she wasn't much of a reader. Anyway, by the time she got up the next morning, I was finishing up the last pages, and I was hooked on Laura Ingalls Wilder. That wouldn't be the last time I stayed up all night reading a great book. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16906596036535136049">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1308403">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1308403" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/51817-ruzmar" class="left imgcol" title="ruzmarì"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1175917504p2/51817.jpg" alt="ruzmarì" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1308403?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 19, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/51817-ruzmar" class="user" title="ruzmarì">ruzmarì</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/8322.Little_House_on_the_Prairie" title="Paperback 9780060885397" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/51817-ruzmar?shelf=juvenilia" class="actionLinkLite">juvenilia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1308403">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1571291571822543722">I scrolled quickly down the page and noticed that nobody has much to say about this novel. What _is_ there to say about Laura Ingalls Wilder's fiction/memoir accounts of growing up in the period of American expansion and homesteading? A lot - at least 7 volumes' worth, in Ingalls Wilder's own series. It's easy to categorize Ingalls Wilder's series as "children's" literature, but her books are also documents of an indomitable feminine spirit, a woman's relation of the American experience in a tim</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1571291571822543722">I scrolled quickly down the page and noticed that nobody has much to say about this novel. What _is_ there to say about Laura Ingalls Wilder's fiction/memoir accounts of growing up in the period of American expansion and homesteading? A lot - at least 7 volumes' worth, in Ingalls Wilder's own series. It's easy to categorize Ingalls Wilder's series as "children's" literature, but her books are also documents of an indomitable feminine spirit, a woman's relation of the American experience in a time when "high" literature focuses only on the cities of the East, dismissing the westward push as fledgling cities of the plain.<br /><br />But there is nothing plain about the Little House books. Through Laura's eyes, we see the 19th century experience firsthand. The advent of the railroad, and early communications technology. The raw human communities that form around open space and the need for solidarity in times of hardship. We see petty jealousies and lifetime friendships, suffering and triumph, death and birth. We see the process of taming the wild land to make a working farm, and the struggle to build a functional home for a growing family, out there in the middle of nothing but prairie. Governmental decisions and big-scale events are mediated through the child's point of view - this is not "hard history," but rather history made intimate, a "this is how we lived it" reel, history that grows more perceptive and subtler as the narrator grows up.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1571291571822543722">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_759938765">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/759938765" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2679341-jane" class="left imgcol" title="Jane"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442635072p2/2679341.jpg" alt="Jane" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/759938765?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 30, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2679341-jane" class="user" title="Jane">Jane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2679341-jane?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer759938765">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11048141423064195983">Where I got the book: my daughter's bookshelf.<br /><br />Finally did it, folks. Read that American childhood classic everyone else but me seems to have read. Of course I didn't grow up in America so I have an excuse!<br /><br />And I liked it. Almost ran upstairs for the next one. Sure, the Indians are portrayed as savages who steal and threaten, and the Ingalls family (who had set up housekeeping illegally in the Indians' territory) make absolutely no attempt to understand or really communicate with them. But that's</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11048141423064195983">Where I got the book: my daughter's bookshelf.<br /><br />Finally did it, folks. Read that American childhood classic everyone else but me seems to have read. Of course I didn't grow up in America so I have an excuse!<br /><br />And I liked it. Almost ran upstairs for the next one. Sure, the Indians are portrayed as savages who steal and threaten, and the Ingalls family (who had set up housekeeping illegally in the Indians' territory) make absolutely no attempt to understand or really communicate with them. But that's a pretty typical portrayal of the mindset of white settlers, who believed in their Manifest Destiny to overrun the land, that the only good Indian was a dead Indian, that they were biologically superior and that they would "improve" the land they lived on. All books written in the 19th and early 20th centuries reflect those unpalatable attitudes; our 21st century attitude is to feel outraged because we consider ourselves superior to our ancestors, but I don't suppose many of us would seriously consider inviting the tribes back into our suburbs. Perhaps our great-grandchildren will.<br /><br />And if the Ingalls family were anything to go by, those settlers were as ballsy as they were naive. They appear to have survived that year on the prairie mostly by dumb luck. If nothing else, this little book gives you an idea of the difficulties and dangers of homesteading and portrays just how frightening the plains must have appeared to the hapless women and children who got dragged into their menfolk's big adventure. I bet Ma Ingalls breathed a huge sigh of relief as they left the Little House behind.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11048141423064195983">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/3983777-michele" class="user" title="Michele">Michele</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3983777-michele?shelf=childrens-historical" class="actionLinkLite">childrens-historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer110850334">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9222525660598337921">I read this book when I was six years old, and then over and over again until I was about ten. I loved it. It inspired my imagination like nothing else until Harry Potter more than thirty years later. For years, I wanted to BE Laura Ingalls Wilder. I loved when the grass grew long and I could pretend it was the prairie. When I was stuck in the outfield during elementary school softball I was imagining I was playing with Mary and Carrie. I read all the books and wrote my own biography of Laura wh</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9222525660598337921">I read this book when I was six years old, and then over and over again until I was about ten. I loved it. It inspired my imagination like nothing else until Harry Potter more than thirty years later. For years, I wanted to BE Laura Ingalls Wilder. I loved when the grass grew long and I could pretend it was the prairie. When I was stuck in the outfield during elementary school softball I was imagining I was playing with Mary and Carrie. I read all the books and wrote my own biography of Laura when I was nine. I was thrilled to discover an actual biography (<a title="Laura: The Life of Laura Ingalls Wilder by Donald Zochert" href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8221.Laura__The_Life_of_Laura_Ingalls_Wilder">Laura: The Life of Laura Ingalls Wilder</a>)! <br /><br />What did I love about the <em>Little House</em> books? I loved Laura. I loved that she felt <em>real</em> emotion. She was bored, frustrated, and jealous. I loved the adventure: the idea of moving slowly across the wilderness with all of my family's belongings in one small wagon was deeply appealing. I was fascinated by the wolves, Indians, and the details of the cabin building. I loved the idea of living <em>in the past.</em> It was years before I thought about how difficult life would be without the conveniences of modern plumbing. I must also mention that I was entranced by idea of wearing a calico-print dress and making my own dolls as well! <br /><br />As I grew older, I grew to love more historical fiction. I loved exploring the past. I fell in love with <a title="Caddie Woodlawn by Carol Ryrie Brink" href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/205821.Caddie_Woodlawn">Caddie Woodlawn</a>, <a title="A Little Princess by Frances Hodgson Burnett" href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3008.A_Little_Princess">A Little Princess</a>, <a title="The Witch of Blackbird Pond by Elizabeth George Speare" href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/703292.The_Witch_of_Blackbird_Pond">The Witch of Blackbird Pond</a>, and, later, <a title="A Lantern in Her Hand by Bess Streeter Aldrich" href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/937020.A_Lantern_in_Her_Hand">A Lantern in Her Hand</a>. I was able to experience the hardships of girls all over the world in different time periods. <a title="Little House on the Prairie by Laura Ingalls Wilder" href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77767.Little_House_on_the_Prairie">Little House on the Prairie</a> introduced me to that world. <br /><br />I have read a variety of criticisms of <a title="The Little House Collection by Laura Ingalls Wilder" href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/114345.The_Little_House_Collection">The Little House Collection</a>. People are bothered by the treatment of Native Americans or the embedded sexism of the family. My response to this complaint is, yes, Ma was a racist, and girls were treated worse than they are today. But Laura doesn't like it! And she wins, in the end. The Ingalls family could not stay in Indian Territory and Laura is the one who becomes an internationally known author, while the rest of the ladylike girls disappear into obscurity. <br /><br />I am now a school librarian, and still recommend these books to my students. The writing is clear and straight forward without being too short or short on detail. There is no doubt that the book must be read in context, but in today's school environment, historical fiction is often the way children begin to learn about history.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9222525660598337921">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_152972216">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/152972216" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/575806-kressel-housman" class="left imgcol" title="Kressel Housman"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1245872830p2/575806.jpg" alt="Kressel Housman" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/152972216?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 15, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/575806-kressel-housman" class="user" title="Kressel Housman">Kressel Housman</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/575806-kressel-housman?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/575806-kressel-housman?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/575806-kressel-housman?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/575806-kressel-housman?shelf=all-time-favorite" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favorite</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/575806-kressel-housman?shelf=little-house" class="actionLinkLite">little-house</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/575806-kressel-housman?shelf=native-american" class="actionLinkLite">native-american</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/575806-kressel-housman?shelf=made-into-movie" class="actionLinkLite">made-into-movie</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer152972216">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15862587808837821782">I am hoping to review all the Little House books in the order I read them, so even though <em>Little House on the Prairie</em> is the second in the series, it was first for me. I was seven years old when I first read it, and my family had just moved from Manhattan to Queens, primarily so that I could attend a better school. I was the best reader in my first grade class in Manhattan, but second grade in Queens was a rude awakening. The kids there were reading chaptered books of more than 100 pages! Amongs</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15862587808837821782">I am hoping to review all the Little House books in the order I read them, so even though <em>Little House on the Prairie</em> is the second in the series, it was first for me. I was seven years old when I first read it, and my family had just moved from Manhattan to Queens, primarily so that I could attend a better school. I was the best reader in my first grade class in Manhattan, but second grade in Queens was a rude awakening. The kids there were reading chaptered books of more than 100 pages! Amongst the girls, Little House was the big favorite, so I jumped right in.<br /><br />To tell the truth, I’m not 100% sure that <em>Little House on the Prairie</em> was my first chaptered book. It may have been <em>Charlotte’s Web</em>. What I do remember clearly is sitting in my class, reading a scene in which Laura is in the covered wagon while Pa is driving it across a river. “I’d love to see this on TV,” I thought. It was 1975. The show was in its second season, but I hadn’t discovered it yet. It wasn’t until after I read my second Little House book that I did discover it, and when I did, it felt like a wish come true!<br /><br />As to the book, though, the line that I’ve remembered best through all these years is: “Perhaps Mary felt sweet and good inside, but Laura didn’t.” That was one of the greatest things about the books: Laura’s struggles with negative emotions were so familiar. But much of the history was lost on me as a child. I re-read what’s left of my old copy this Shabbos, and as an adult, I could see the broader political context. I’m sure I didn’t understand way back when that the Indians held their jamboree because the government had told the settlers to leave Indian Territory. And when the Ingalls family did leave at the end of the book, I don’t think I understood that it had to with the government's treaty with the Native Americans. I just thought that traveling around the prairie in a covered wagon was what pioneers did.<br /><br />But it’s a classic book and a lifetime favorite. I do think, though, that the kids who read it should be given background information so that they can appreciate the history. There are homeschoolers’ guides for teaching the book. Perhaps school libraries across the country should invest in them. Kids enjoy the books because of the quality of the writing, but a little information from a teacher or parent can make the book even more meaningful.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15862587808837821782">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_146205408">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/146205408" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/677502-kellie" class="left imgcol" title="Kellie"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Kellie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/146205408?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 06, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/677502-kellie" class="user" title="Kellie">Kellie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/677502-kellie?shelf=children" class="actionLinkLite">children</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/677502-kellie?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/677502-kellie?shelf=bibliotherapy" class="actionLinkLite">bibliotherapy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/677502-kellie?shelf=read-aloud" class="actionLinkLite">read-aloud</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/677502-kellie?shelf=western" class="actionLinkLite">western</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer146205408">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17321084399132068842">I give this book five stars with a huge caveat - it should not be read by a child without adult guidance and discussion, and is probably most appropriate for children ages 8 and up. There is a lot of blatant racism in this story, as well as a lot of more subtle language problems. It is clear to me that Laura, writing as an adult, understood the problems with what her family was doing at the time - moving into Indian Territory - and that she to some extent understood the perspective of the Indian</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17321084399132068842">I give this book five stars with a huge caveat - it should not be read by a child without adult guidance and discussion, and is probably most appropriate for children ages 8 and up. There is a lot of blatant racism in this story, as well as a lot of more subtle language problems. It is clear to me that Laura, writing as an adult, understood the problems with what her family was doing at the time - moving into Indian Territory - and that she to some extent understood the perspective of the Indians. But she was also a product of her times and that is reflected in her language. All of that being said, this book has been a tremendous tool in our family to discuss racism. Laura questions her parents a lot in a way that makes sense to kids, "Why do we live in Indian Territory if you don't like Indians?...Why does the government keep making the Indians move? Won't that make them angry?" <br /><br />The Native Americans in this book behave in a way that does not make sense to Laura and her family which led to a lot of discussion in our house about cultural conventions and cultural differences. For example, many of the Indians simply walked right into the Ingalls' home. Some Indians were also rude and clearly trying to be intimidating. Others were kind in spite of all the problems with the Ingalls living there. We talked about how that was how it is with people everywhere. Ma is blatantly prejudiced against Indians. Pa makes several comments that make me cringe, but overall he had a pretty progressive view for the time. All of these were big catalysts for discussion when we read the book aloud.<br /><br />I did do some editing while I was reading. This was our evening read-aloud which my four and a half year old was often present for. She wasn't really old enough to get all of this, and I don't recommend this book for her age group. I did some editing for her and my sensitive seven-year-old (the Ingalls have a neighbor who likes to say, "The only good Indian is a dead Indian" and that was just too much for them). My nine-year-old and I had a lot of great discussions about this book (sometimes after the other two went to bed) and I think she really gets it. I think it was very eye-opening to her to see characters she likes and is attached to - like Caroline - have such blatant flaws. I also think it was eye-opening to her herself - I have heard a few comments and questions from her about Native Americans that had disturbed me, and after reading this she was clearly outraged on their behalf and has become interested in learning more about Native American history and culture.<br /><br />To me, this is like the elementary school Huck Finn.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17321084399132068842">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_32005048">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32005048" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1240145-margaret" class="left imgcol" title="Margaret"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1213846826p2/1240145.jpg" alt="Margaret" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/32005048?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 04, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1240145-margaret" class="user" title="Margaret">Margaret</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer32005048">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5723925777222152155">Laura’s family decides to go west because the area in which they live in Minnesota in the big woods is getting too populated and scaring the game away. So they pack up all of their belongings and leave their little house and go toward the prairie land. They find a piece of land in the prairie and start over. They encounter trials and joys along the way. They make some neighbor friends who live several miles away. They are in Indian Territory and deal with nice Indians and the not-so-nice Indians</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5723925777222152155">Laura’s family decides to go west because the area in which they live in Minnesota in the big woods is getting too populated and scaring the game away. So they pack up all of their belongings and leave their little house and go toward the prairie land. They find a piece of land in the prairie and start over. They encounter trials and joys along the way. They make some neighbor friends who live several miles away. They are in Indian Territory and deal with nice Indians and the not-so-nice Indians.<br /><br />What I love most about this book is the family’s ability to make a home of love wherever they may be. Whether it is traveling in their covered wagon to find a home or camping out next to the plot of land where they are going to build their house, their life always seems so cozy and filled with love. Laura’s parents are very gentle people. I also love hearing about the simple joys in Laura’s life. Like when she found colorful beads in a deserted Indian camp or how exciting it was to receive a cup, a candy cane and cookie from Santa Clause. She was so grateful for these simple things and it makes me wish that we lived in a simpler time and where didn’t have to have so much “stuff.”</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5723925777222152155">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/34868824?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1572676-keli" class="user" title="Keli">Keli</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1572676-keli?shelf=lis-565" class="actionLinkLite">lis-565</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1572676-keli?shelf=juvenile" class="actionLinkLite">juvenile</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1572676-keli?shelf=historical" class="actionLinkLite">historical</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1572676-keli?shelf=tween" class="actionLinkLite">tween</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer34868824">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer541624389111565448"><strong>Synopsis</strong><br /><br />Part autobiography, part fiction, this book tells the story of a pioneer family settling in Indian Territory in the plains of the American Southwest.<br /><br /><strong>Review</strong><br /><br />This American classic can problematic to modern readers. Ma's meek demeanor and the frequent reminder that "children should be seen and not heard" are not likely to resonate with youth of the 21st century and are likely more fiction than autobiography. Additionally, while the book had a clear beginning and end, the middle was more a se</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText541624389111565448"><strong>Synopsis</strong><br /><br />Part autobiography, part fiction, this book tells the story of a pioneer family settling in Indian Territory in the plains of the American Southwest.<br /><br /><strong>Review</strong><br /><br />This American classic can problematic to modern readers. Ma's meek demeanor and the frequent reminder that "children should be seen and not heard" are not likely to resonate with youth of the 21st century and are likely more fiction than autobiography. Additionally, while the book had a clear beginning and end, the middle was more a series of events rather than a clear narrative. However, the detailed descriptions of pioneer daily life, perils and methods of creating creature comforts are interesting in a documentary sort of way. It's almost instructive in it's passages on the building of a log cabin and rocking chair. The constant optimism sometimes feels refreshing and at other times feels grating.<br /><br />One surprising element of the book is the way it addresses the issue of Native Americans. At the beginning of the book, Pa decides to settle several miles into Indian Territory with assurances from the U.S. government that it will soon be opened to settlers. This creates tension with the Osage Indians living in the area. The feeling of manifest destiny and fear of Native Americans is realistic for white pioneers at the time. However, the portrayals of Native Americans tend toward stereotypes of noble savage and wildman. The tension over land and culture is felt periodically throughout the book and brings about the eventual climax, though it is simplified through a six year old's eyes. <br /><br />This book is a must for any juvenile collection. It is entertaining and an American Classic. Some elements of the book are timeless, others are a bit more dated or downright offensive when looked at through modern eyes.<br /><br /><strong>Critical Reviews</strong><br /><br />No critical reviews available.<br /><br /><strong>Additional Bibiliographic Information</strong><br />Published by HarperCollins in 1935<br /><br />335 Pages<br /><br />Ages 9-12</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="541624389111565448">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer187517438">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3105056237263955619">I read this to my three girls, ages six, seven, and nine. My husband also listened each night, as I always read two chapters before bedtime. And in fact, he got very upset with me one night when I was unable to read! <br /><br />I remember reading these as a girl and loving them. But I didn't expect to enjoy them so much as an adult. The entire family looked forward to reading time each night. Laura's story is told with the experience of an adult, but at the same time she manages to tell it from the perspec</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3105056237263955619">I read this to my three girls, ages six, seven, and nine. My husband also listened each night, as I always read two chapters before bedtime. And in fact, he got very upset with me one night when I was unable to read! <br /><br />I remember reading these as a girl and loving them. But I didn't expect to enjoy them so much as an adult. The entire family looked forward to reading time each night. Laura's story is told with the experience of an adult, but at the same time she manages to tell it from the perspective of a child. She is at once in awe of the Indians and scared of their warriors. She describes the animal life with the absolute glee and innocence of a young girl. The wide open spaces are magical, and every day brings new discoveries.<br /><br />As a parent, I can't imagine moving my kids away from family and civilization the way that Laura's parents did. But she got to experience some amazing things living out on the prairie. We are looking forward to reading Almanzo's story in the next book, Farmer Boy. I'm so very glad that these books are being enjoyed by my girls as much as I enjoyed them when I was a kid! And I love that in the day of technology, they still want to take the time to hear this classic American story! Great family time.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3105056237263955619">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_914083585">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/914083585" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/11758600-victoria-scott" class="left imgcol" title="Victoria Scott"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1418532331p2/11758600.jpg" alt="Victoria Scott" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/914083585?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 27, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/11758600-victoria-scott" class="user" title="Victoria Scott">Victoria Scott</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/11758600-victoria-scott?shelf=childhood" class="actionLinkLite">childhood</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11758600-victoria-scott?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11758600-victoria-scott?shelf=105-challenge-14" class="actionLinkLite">105-challenge-14</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11758600-victoria-scott?shelf=physically-own" class="actionLinkLite">physically-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer914083585">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5378038711461900111">Compared to the first book, this one was a lot more interesting. And, again, it had all those great memories of me reading this when I was a kid!<br />The book started with the family moving away from the only home they'd known, and making a new life on the prairie. I loved reading that part. It wasn't like how they were when they were living in a house. There, it was all about how they sewed, or how they played with their dolls. When they were just starting out their new lives, they had to build ever</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5378038711461900111">Compared to the first book, this one was a lot more interesting. And, again, it had all those great memories of me reading this when I was a kid!<br />The book started with the family moving away from the only home they'd known, and making a new life on the prairie. I loved reading that part. It wasn't like how they were when they were living in a house. There, it was all about how they sewed, or how they played with their dolls. When they were just starting out their new lives, they had to build everything from scratch. It was interesting reading about how they made their own house. It was impressive that they did that, and built a well, and on and on. Pa even managed to build a rocking chair, because he was "too sore to work". That was his idea of a casual weekend. My idea of a casual weekend is blobbing out in my pjs in bed. This family never stopped working. <br />I liked seeing how they treated the Indians, too. Ma was completely racist. She was so afraid of them, even though they'd done nothing wrong. Pa was sure that they would kick them out of their country, so that the whites could settle there. Laura spent half the trip saying how much she wanted to see a papoose, an Indian baby. Then when she saw one, she wanted to keep it for her own. Luckily, Pa and Laura actually treated them like people. Pa told Ma countless times not to be afraid of them. But he was one of the different ones. It was crazy seeing how separated they were, and how superior the whites believed themselves to be.<br />Again, this wasn't as good as I remembered it. It's quite a short book, luckily, or I think I would've got bored. But it is nice to get to go back to my childhood through these books again</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5378038711461900111">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer221363250">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3765374665366154464">Little House on the Prairie by Laura Ingalls Wilder is an exquisite set of books that I cherished growing up. Read until they were dog-eared, this series has to be one of my childhood favorites. A story about a young girl growing up on the frontier, it was so popular they made it into a T.V. series even though the series didn't do it justice. Stories as a young girl I could relate to, the mean girl in town, fights with my sisters, and just the struggles of everyday life of any family. The love M</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3765374665366154464">Little House on the Prairie by Laura Ingalls Wilder is an exquisite set of books that I cherished growing up. Read until they were dog-eared, this series has to be one of my childhood favorites. A story about a young girl growing up on the frontier, it was so popular they made it into a T.V. series even though the series didn't do it justice. Stories as a young girl I could relate to, the mean girl in town, fights with my sisters, and just the struggles of everyday life of any family. The love Ma and Pa had for each other showed through so much so, that even today I can still see Caroline's eye's sparkling bright blue as Pa whirled her around the dance floor. This series is a perfect example of a story well told. When you're there in Laura's life so much that you can feel her fear when in trouble, or you can taste the penny candy on Christmas, that's a story. I have no doubt this will be a children's classic for years to come. I highly recommend it. <br /><br />Note: Reading again with my son, what a joy to see him loving these too! We've even started watching the tv series along with the reading!<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://classicsdefined.com/littlehouse.html">ClassicsDefined.com<br /></a></span>

Saya rasa agak 'familiar' dengan tajuk ini. Mungkin saya pernah menontonnya satu ketika dahulu dan baru saya ketahui ianya adalah dari sebuah buku. <br /><br />Kecekalan sebuah keluarga yang berhijrah dari Big Wood menuju ke satu tempat baru yang dikenali sebagai Indian Territory. <br /><br />Membaca helaian demi helaian pelbagai emosi dirasai. <div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_83766861">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/83766861" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2355996-logan" class="left imgcol" title="Logan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1243405864p2/2355996.jpg" alt="Logan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/83766861?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 23, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2355996-logan" class="user" title="Logan">Logan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2355996-logan?shelf=historical" class="actionLinkLite">historical</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2355996-logan?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer83766861">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3047287497244707657">We *loved* Little House in the Big Woods. The community there was wonderful and many of the chapters started out scary but turned out funny. I can't say the same for this second book in the series. In this book, Pa feels compelled to move away from family and friends to stake his claim in "Indian Territory," hoping that the Indians will be forced to move further west and then Pa will own the land he's "settled." I was prepared for discussions about racism, land ownership, power, and what it mean</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3047287497244707657">We *loved* Little House in the Big Woods. The community there was wonderful and many of the chapters started out scary but turned out funny. I can't say the same for this second book in the series. In this book, Pa feels compelled to move away from family and friends to stake his claim in "Indian Territory," hoping that the Indians will be forced to move further west and then Pa will own the land he's "settled." I was prepared for discussions about racism, land ownership, power, and what it means to "settle" land. But even though I don't edit what I read to Logan, I had a hard time getting my mouth to form the words "the only good Indian is a dead Indian." This sentiment was popular during the push west and is voiced by a neighbor and Pa rightly disputes it, but it's pretty hard to take regardless and I stopped right there to talk about it. I know that this is a book written in the 1930's by an older woman who is remembering back to what her (often incorrect) impressions were as a young child, but it's hard for me to get that across to a 5-year-old. I don't think he's enjoying this one as much either. There's not much funny about it and there's a lot more danger. We may not continue the series. I'll have to read the 3rd book first and see what I think. In the meantime, I think we'll go back to magical stories. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3047287497244707657">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/109559898?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 14, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/949924-maria" class="user" title="Maria">Maria</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer109559898">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2379272486518430510">A wonderful look at what life was like on the prairie in the late 19th century. I particularly enjoyed the descriptions of building a log cabin and the bed and chair. Just thinking about a day in the life of Charles Wilder is enough to make a modern type blanch. The men and women then must have been incredibly hardy. He just kept working ... and working ... and working ... long after I had collapsed from exhaustion reading about it. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1460108249">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1460108249" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8243269-mary" class="left imgcol" title="Mary"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450559998p2/8243269.jpg" alt="Mary" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1460108249?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 10, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8243269-mary" class="user" title="Mary">Mary</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16085376-little-house-on-the-prairie" title="Paperback " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/8243269-mary?shelf=ya" class="actionLinkLite">ya</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/8243269-mary?shelf=used-bookstore-find" class="actionLinkLite">used-bookstore-find</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1460108249">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5713031408746074339">Book 2 in the Little House series sees some big changes in the lives of the Ingalls family. Charles becomes unhappy as the woods of Wisconsin become more and more crowded so he decides that they will move west. <br /><br />The first part of the story chronicles the road trip. Imagine packing up everything you can take in a covered wagon and traveling for weeks. Each night you have to stop and make camp - build a fire, cook dinner and sleep in the great outdoors. After a few scares, they finally reach Oklaho</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5713031408746074339">Book 2 in the Little House series sees some big changes in the lives of the Ingalls family. Charles becomes unhappy as the woods of Wisconsin become more and more crowded so he decides that they will move west. <br /><br />The first part of the story chronicles the road trip. Imagine packing up everything you can take in a covered wagon and traveling for weeks. Each night you have to stop and make camp - build a fire, cook dinner and sleep in the great outdoors. After a few scares, they finally reach Oklahoma and decide they have found their new home.<br /><br />Charles begins building a house and barn. The descriptions of the building process were fascinating. When you run out of nails, you can't just go to Lowe's and pick up a box. You have to improvise. They dig a well, plant a garden and become friends with their neighbors. When the Ingalls arrived, it looked like there was an old Indian camp nearby but noone was there.<br /><br />Then, they came back. The Osage. <br /><br />Reading with modern and grown-up sensibilities is interesting to say the least. I'm not going to spend time giving you a social and political commentary about the way Indians were treated. All I will say is that reading this story from Laura's perspective made me think. As written, Charles Ingalls is a good man. He is quick to point out to his family and his new friends in Oklahoma that the Indians seemed to mean them no harm and they should basically respect each other. The only time he truly showed fear of them was the night of the War Cries. (So well written! I got goose bumps.) Before leaving Wisconsin, he researched to ensure the territory he was moving into was open for settlement by the government. From his viewpoint, it was available land to claim. Obviously, the Osage saw that differently.<br /><br />I guess I can't spoil a book this old so here goes. The ending was sad. When Charles found out the government had signed a treaty with the Osage saying that the settlers had to leave, he was furious (the first time we really see him lose his temper). His anger stemmed from the fact that he thought he was clear to move to this spot. He wasn't angry with the Osage but with the government. And, when told that soldiers will be coming to move them out, he takes a deep breath and tells his family they are leaving the following morning and won't wait to be driven from their home. Modern, adult Mary thought, "Good, this is Osage land." But a small part of my heart was broken. The Ingalls' blood, sweat and tears had gone into building this home with their own hands and they had to leave almost everything. Charles handled it better than me: <br /><br />Ma sighed gently and said, "A whole year gone, Charles." But Pa answered, cheerfully: "What's a year amount to? We have all the time there is."<br /><br />5/5 stars. <br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5713031408746074339">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1077545958">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1077545958" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2557595-kate" class="left imgcol" title="Kate"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1265162185p2/2557595.jpg" alt="Kate" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1077545958?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 09, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2557595-kate" class="user" title="Kate">Kate</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1077545958">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17068942291309686252">I am rereading the Little House series, mostly because there is a new book coming out this Fall that is Laura Ingalls Wilder's previously unpublished autobiography with details showing that quite a bit of the Little House series is fiction. For example, it appears that Jack the dog was "sold with the ponies" early on and much of his wonderful storyline is fiction....!!<br /><br />I first read this series in elementary school, again in high school and again to my kids 20 some years ago. Now I am 60+ years ol</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17068942291309686252">I am rereading the Little House series, mostly because there is a new book coming out this Fall that is Laura Ingalls Wilder's previously unpublished autobiography with details showing that quite a bit of the Little House series is fiction. For example, it appears that Jack the dog was "sold with the ponies" early on and much of his wonderful storyline is fiction....!!<br /><br />I first read this series in elementary school, again in high school and again to my kids 20 some years ago. Now I am 60+ years old and seeing things from a different, and often disturbing, angle. I am not happy to discover that this book is racist; Native Americans are depicted as terrifying animals.<br /><br />Pa's survival skills never cease to amaze. He can make anything out of wood and is a terrific hunter. Ma also has amazing home skills while trying to keep her girls refined and repressed. Pa makes all the decisions. <br /><br />Pa's encounter with a pack of 50 wolves is worth a read next time you are at the library. <br /><br />Ma must have been so nervous every time Charles takes off to go hunting or into town and doesn't come back as expected. She knows how to work all sorts of magic with a fireplace and in the home. And how to give birth by herself! She does apparently know how to use a gun and a pistol but we never hear of her actually shooting.<br /><br />Despite complaining, I enjoy this book and would love to read it again someday. But while reading it out loud, say to a grandchild, I would certainly have conversations about the attitudes towards the role of women and the terrible attitudes towards Native Americans.<br /><br />NOTE: <br />I snobbily never liked the Little House tv show since it was not at all true to the books. Also, I will only read the versions of these books that are illustrated by GARTH WILLIAMS. I just love his drawings and he helped make the stories come alive over all these years.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17068942291309686252">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
